RATING: 6
7 plays / 4,5,6 players

LIKE: Quick, easy to learn. It's fun to push-your-luck when auctioning, since the last player must pay full.

DISLIKE: The little coins only come in one denomination, so it's hard to count out 21 coins. The bidding can be predictable at times. Some player order binding when auctioning.

OVERALL: A classic filler, but very light.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
2009-01-12
Chaotic but great fun for 15 mins

2016-05-13
For Sale is a bidding game that is very easy to approach. Rather simple and quick to teach but still offering tough decisions. The game is quite chaotic but mostly skill based since you can try to outguess your opponents.

Lacks the depth required for higher ratings, though. Nevertheless, there is plenty to ponder for a 15 minute filler so there is no way you can call the game shallow. However, after some time your plays start to follow certain patterns and it takes really outrageous opponents to thwart you. Thus I am a bit concerned that the optimal play is a bit too hard coded into the game and the number of different situations the game throws at you is limited.

I would not be too concerned though. If enjoyed responsibly For Sale offers years of fun.

RATING: 8
Wow!  Great little game that causes much amusement and laughter. It's great how the differences in property values in the first half of the game, and cheques in the second half of the game can cause you palpitations and influence your decisions about what to bid.  There's a fair amount of bluffing in it, too, and there's nothing better than selling a cardboard box to the bank for several thousand dollars.  

You've got poker-like bidding in the first half of the game and blind bidding in the second half - lovely!  And it's so elegant and pretty to look at that you're just dying to get the game out when sessions allow.

Interesting game, easy mechanics and fast playtime.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
3-6(5)

Quick, easy to understand, and reasonably fun. There's a psychological element, but also just a lot of guesswork.

The first phase has an element of chicken to it as well as rationing and relative evaluation. Getting a 30 is nice, but less so if everybody else got something in the 20s. Getting a 15 is nice if all the other lots are really low-valued, but you're still left with only a 15, certainly not a guaranteed winning lot.

The second phase then is where the odd guesswork comes in. Sometimes you're convinced that top properties will come out and you play big, but turns out everybody else chose that round of checks to dump their junk. And vice versa.

Interesting, but moreso from a "how things play out" perspective than from a strategic angle.

There are a lot of decisions to make, but ultimately it feels like they could just go either way (on certain lots, say 28 / 25 / 21 / 5 / 2, people might play aggressively or just dump lowbies), and justifiably. Also, gamers might view this one as a "gateway", but games like this and Ticket to Ride, to "rookies", qualify as the real deal, and that's meaningful.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Quick and fun game that is more about reading your opponents and adapting your game plan constantly to keep up with the game. Although this is a random card draw game it is really an auction game focused on bidding wars with limited resources. Bid to high early and you will be broke for the later rounds. Bid to low and you are left with worthless properties for round two. There is no guarantee that skill and reading people will win you the game but it will get you a very good chance.

For Sale ‐ Ravensburger edition (3-5p) (1997)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A wonderfully designed simple/filler auction game that is divided in two phases. In the 1st phase, property-cards are auctioned off and later used to buy contract/money cards in the 2nd phase. But what really sets this game apart is that players must consider the actions of other players; often, you want to bid the perfect amount so that you end up in 2nd-place, which guarantees you the 2nd-highest property for only 1/2 your bid. Furthermore, by tracking what cards have been purchased/played, you can make more meaningful decisions in the 2nd-phase (blind-bidding auction).

This game is short, fun, and very intuitive. It's going to remain in my collection for some time.

RATING: 7
Pretty refreshing game. It has been a while since we last played this and we pretty much forgotten the rules. Was introduce to this first by a friend, then bought a copy of my own this year from Hong Kong.

I feel that the format for the 3-4 players and the 5-6 players is quite different. For the 3-4 players setup, you have to remove 6 properties cards and 6 cheques, which gives more uncertainty since you are not aware if there is going to be a level 30 property or 2 cheques of 15,000 each.

Still it is enjoyable as a 5-6 players game, just that the game will be slightly more predictable.

RATING: 8
This game seems to get a little better every time I play it.  At first I was less than impressed, but now I think there's really a great game in there.  The game is amazingly fast, but it seems to be loaded with difficult decisions.  Players will be challenged to decide when it is advantageous to throw in a lot of cash, and when it makes more sense to bow out.

Then in the second round it becomes a game of reading your opponents and deciding when the best moment is to slip in those cheap houses.  It seems that every game I've played of For Sale has been close and comes down to a matter of a few points.  This kind of tension in a fast filler game is priceless.  Although I wasn't able to see it after my first game, now I'm sure that this game is a real gem.

RATING: 7
Awesome filler game.  It's an almost guaranteed hit with non-gamers -- nearly every time I've brought it out, a second (or third or fourth) playing has been demanded.  You can blow through a game in fifteen minutes INCLUDING teaching the rules.  Half of the game is a simple auction with a clever twist; the other half is multiplayer rock-paper-scissors.

Unfortunately, this game will NOT work with some gamers.  I introduced it to my heavier gaming buddies and their reaction was, "Um...was that it?"

RATING: 6
For a bidding game, I was quite surprised to find that I didn't mind this one. I rate it about like High Society, but I probably like it a little better. Not only is it mercifully short and sweet, it also has an element of abstractness that seemed to help me - If you didn't do great in the first phase, you have a chance to recover a bit in the resell phase.

None of our regular gaming friends have this, so I kept picking this up and putting it back down for over a year in the store. However, I just received it as a gift from my 2007 BGG Secret Santa. Yay! It does make a great little filler game, and we are a little short of those (and I'm getting tired of 6 Nimmt! for the time being). And I just noticed that it's also a 6-player game, which makes it even more of a score, since we've had occasion to want a couple more of those.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
This is brilliant. It's a filler alright, but a great one. The double blind auction concept is superb. First off, you bid for houses with your cash. That's fair enough. Lots of scope to screw your neighbour are built in to this as when you drop out, you pay half your bid and take the lowest valued house (1-30). Winner has to pay full price. Then if that wasn't enough, you bid for cheques ($1,000 to $15,000) with your houses!

This is a really fast game, takes 15 minutes to explain, set up, play, put away again. So you just have to play it twice.

RATING: 7.5
This game is fun.  I like the tension created by the first round betting, as you either risk paying full price, or pay half for a lesser card. RATING: 6
Played it two times with a party of six. With that many players turn order becomes so important. That, combined with not knowing which sets of buildings turn up the next round make it hard to control. It's as unpredictable as a volatile stock market. How much do you invest? Can you skip the next round, or should you save some money?

What saves this game are the bouts of extreme luck and fortune that can happen and the frustration and hilarity that ensues among players. And it plays quick. Okay to play as a conclusion of a game night I guess, but that's about it.

RATING: 7
12.12.10  I've played this enough now that I can say it's a solid 7 for me.  I like it, but the one part that I don't care for as much is that the first-round auctions aren't granular enough, due to only having $14 to bid.  I know that's part of the design.  It also seems VERY dependent on seating order; how far you are from the person who starts the bid can determine your destiny more than I'd like.  However, it is short, fun, and you can do more than you think with a lousy hand of cards.  A good filler, but not the Holy Grail of fillers some think it is.

One of my absolute favorite filler games. It has simple rules that create tough choices. The bidding is tense, and the blind bidding in the second round even more so. I enjoy having two rounds that use different mechanics (first round as open auctioning, and the second as bluffing/blind auction). Can be a bit luck-based, as being in a certain spot in turn order can get you more valuable properties, or screw you in the bidding. For a 20-minute, large group (5-6 player) filler, this takes the cake.

RATING: 8
This is a favorite filler of many, and I do see why as the game play is quick, easy to teach but still offers some “tough” decisions. I like the first portion of the game more than the second as you need more skill and must evaluate all the cards out there; in the second half of the game, you kind of make blind bids which is a feature I'm not very fond of. But I guess it can lead to a lot of laughter which is great for a filler game (like the one time my brother used his 28 and got a 3 - d'oh!)

I sold my copy as I am not really a big fan of filler games and I think there are better short filler games (like, uhm, No Thanks, High Society, Guillotine and others). If I could get my hands on the tiny version, I would probably purchase it again. EDIT: which I have donw now. The tiny version is superior if you're in need of shelf space like me! I've played it 10+ times more since I acquired that, and I must say that I've grown more and more fond of For Sale. It's definitely one of the best fillers out there (though I still slightly prefer No Thanks and Ave Caesar).

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For Sale is an excellent, "workhorse" filler auction game that handle a range of audiences. I like auctions to begin with, and For Sales offers a two-stage auction: players first bid around the table, with people choosing houses as they drop out, and then blind bid their hand of houses, selling them for a range of prices. In essences, players and buying houses and then trying to flip 'em for a profit.

Regarding theme, you gotta love the sense of humor, with residences ranging from a cardboard box and a latrine all the way up to sky scapers and a space station.

New players will stumble through the first hand, but then once the light bulb goes on, the game is fast and fluid. I've been able to teach this game to non-gamers, but it's got just enough meat for gamers as well. After 20+ plays, it's easy to consider For Sale a good filler-weight game with staying power in my collection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very quick playing auction game where in the first round you use your $18,000 to buy houses. Buyer pays full price while the others play half of their bid rounded up so you can get a house for free. Once all the houses are distributed you sell your houses for checks. Highest numbered house gets the highest check and so on. Add up the checks you've won and highest total wins the game. So short and so simple and really reminds me of Biblios in terms of filler auction games. The only difference is Biblios plays 2 well, while this needs at least 4 in my mind, but is still a lot of fun when you have more people.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
My go-to auction filler.  You can teach the game in 2 minutes and play a game in about 10 minutes.
First phase is the auction phase where you bid for properties that all have a number value 1-30.
Second phase has cards with dollar values.  Every player secretly plays one of the properties they bought, then you compare values.  Highest numbered property takes home the most money.  Repeat until every has played all of their properties.

RATING: 8
I'm not sure yet, but this might be better than High Society by a nose, in a very similar niche for a game.  Its a short auction filler.  For High Society a lot of the tension comes from the variable ending and the need to NOT be the person with the least money left.  In For Sale, the tension comes from not wanting to be the first one to flinch during the bidding.  There is a lot more psychology to the bidding in For Sale, to me.  High Society is more analytical, For Sale more of a 'press-your-luck' gambling feel.  Then, after you've done a great job with getting good deals on properties, you may still squander it all as you sell them to the bank if you don't 'play your cards right'.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A fine auction game filler that compares favourably to High Society but plays even faster. The game runs in two rounds, one is an English bidding round to acquire property the second is a blind offering round to resell it, hopefully at a profit. The auction is less interesting to me than the one in High Society because of the lack of a hook, but the fact that this plays out in 10 minutes flat (whereas HS is more like 15) makes it very fun and replayable. 

The blind round is not as luck-based as some people would say. Even if you don't know what other people are going to bid specifically, you can sometimes win this game just by being cagey with your use of the cards and reacting to the spread of the checks that are available. Always look for groupings where the variation between high and low is not that big and then drop your crap off. Watch and see what cards are being used and try to squeak out extra money that way. I'm not arguing this is Go or anything approaching that, but the blind rounds are done just about perfectly here for my tastes.

A good filler, but I'm definitely starting to burn out on it. It is frequently compared to High Society, and I personally prefer the Knizia game because I think its two types of auction (standard and the agonizing reverse) are each more interesting than either of the round types here. HS is not any more skill-based than FS on the balance though, because the winner is heavily dependent on when the game ends, and players can only make educated guesses about when that is going to happen. FS is definitely the more accessible of the two.

RATING: 8
Nice filler that actually has a good bit of strategy to it. The game consists of two rounds. The first round you are using your starting money to purchase homes. Ideally you want to acquire the highest numbered homes. During the second round you are using the homes to sell for money, and obviously you want the highest valued money for each sale. A game takes less than 15 minutes - definitely worth your time.

RATING: 7
For Sale really is an almost ideal filler game, with a focused, streamlined design that is easy to teach and learn, and that offers just enough chaos to give a new player a shot at winning and enough decisions to satisfy a veteran. That is, as long as it's played in the proper mindset - casually and quickly, with the game length never topping 20 minutes or so and the outcome never taken too seriously. Beyond that, and the lightness of the design and the inherent randomness of the blind bid second half begins to overstay their welcome.

If I did have a complaint, it would be that money actually feels just a little too tight in the first half of the game, and so the bidding never really opens up as much as it seems to want to. This makes player order a pretty big factor in how the first half plays out, which is not my favorite mechanic for a filler game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.92746
The biggest merit to the game is it's short length - ideal as a filler, appetiser or a goodbye-game.

Ultimately a game of evaluating how much a property or money card is worth to you (and others), the two phases are almost two distinct games - sharing no rules - but both revolve around auctions and also thematically back each other to simulate the wheeling and dealing of a property tycoon.

In the first half of the game, going out last seemed nearly always a terrible option - usually, 3rd (out of 5) seemed the optimal place to be, depending on the bid. (winner pays in full, loser pays nothing, others pay half (rounded down)) As soon as the 3rd bidder drops out, though, the next will follow (to avoid being the auction winner) so it seemed as much about predicting others' moves as it was about deciding the value of a card.

In the 2nd phase, I started with a 9, hoping to win 3rd place (managing to do so) and again the game became about predicting how much others would value the cards - thus what they would place down - and playing something in the middle.

No single auction felt game-changing and given the lack of any 'big' choices (just a bunch of smaller ones), it sped along at a good rate. Simultaneously, though, there was clearly some strategy to be employed.

Given the brevity, I'd definitely like another game of this soon-ish.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Rated in relation to other Filler games. This game is very approachable and simple to teach, but it is interesting enough to keep my interest after repeated plays. Over the course of 15+ plays I have rarely lost, so I am comfortable in asserting that this game does reward skill. Being able to read other players and predict their plays and/or bids is crucial to winning this game, and understanding the best ways to play all of the various possible sets of cards given your expectations of your opponents actions during the second phase of the game allows superior players to routinely beat new players. It is a game of valuation and prediction, with elements akin to Poker, Magic: The Gathering, and fighting games like Street Fighter. Players who are good at mind-games (predicting your opponents plays, understanding their valuation of the various cards available, etc) will find success in this game. People who aren't good at that will feel like the game takes no skill and is arbitrary and random. (Much like people often complain that Poker takes no skill and is totally random.)

Overall, excellent Filler. Fairy Tale is the only Filler that routinely competes with it for time at the table.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.9
[June 2016 - 9.9]
I've played nearly 100 times now and it's the near-perfect game. What's great is that even though one can play a lot and developed his/her strategies, that's all thrown out the window when you play against new players. You have no idea how they'll play -- and since so much of this game is dependent on guessing what others will do, you can feel like a fish out of water. Fast... but such great roaring reaction and emotion.

[January 2015 - 9.6]
This little game is near the top of my rankings at the moment, and while that seems crazy, it really puts on a performance. There are many reasons to like it (see below), but No. 1 on my list is that it elicits such great emotions (ups and downs) in short bursts as you reveal your properties to the market in the second round. You can play the second-best card in the game and get screwed. You can play the second-worst card in the game and come away a big winner. That might sound too random for people, but it's not. You really have to think what your opponents are going to play, and that's where the emotions build up as your choosing and then revealing all your cards at the same time. Those moments are pure bliss, even if you're on the losing end, because you just laugh about it. The first round also has its moments, especially when the bids start to go high. It's a very easy game to teach, and it's very portable. There's also plenty of strategy in gauging what to bid based on how many rounds are left, what cards have already shown up and what's still to come. Pretty much everyone I've played For Sale with -- from newbie to hardcore gamer -- says the same thing after a few rounds: "What's this game called again? I'm buying it."

RATING: 8.5
I've been playing this more lately as my kids have taken a real liking to it and I've come to very much appreciate what this game has to offer. It's light and quick with minimal rules overhead, but you really do need to play your fellow players and try for read what you think they are going to do. Despite its apparent simplicity, it is also variable: every group of cards will have a slightly different distribution of cards and that affects the bidding immensely. Auction/bidding games often stress me out, but this is a gem.

The stressful highest-bidder-pays-a-lot coupled with the take-the-lower-prize-and-get-compensated factors are what makes Taj Maal such a great game as well! But this game compresses most of the fun and a fraction of the time and complexity as Knizia's Taj Mahal.

RATING: 6
Idea:
First bid on different houses and then sell them for maximum profit.

Mechanism:
First turn over houses in sets and each round you get 1 house (cards numbered 1-40 or so). Then turn over set of 'money' and secretly decide which house you put for sale, biggest house gets to pick money first.

to who:
Easy to learn, somewhat same idea as on Modern art for example. Houses are nicely named like 'sewers', 'dog house' and 'mansion' depending on how good they are.

RATING: 7.5
A great filler game for six people.  It goes by very fast since you are essentially playing to somewhat different 10 minute games sequentially.  Simple rules, but a surprising amount of tension from the bluffing that occurs during auction.  Also great for teaching kids money management skills!  

However, I absolutely hate the corny artwork and poor production values of the cardboard ‘coins’ of the 2008 Eagle-Gryphon Games’ version.  Some of the fan-made print-and-play versions are better than this version.  The box is also ridiculously oversized!  I liked the older versions much better where the currency cards actually looked like miniature banker’s cheques; thematically it also made much more sense and I’m not sure what the new currency cards as supposed to be now???  Lets get some better artwork and some actual plastic coins in a new edition!   

However, my rating here is based on the actual gameplay, not the artwork or poor production value.

RATING: 6
For Sale is an filler auction game that plays with a large compliment of players in two distinct halves. The first half is trying to acquire property with the initial starting money each player has. Money not used in the phase will contribute towards having the most money (which wins you the game), but has no function in the second half. In the second half of the game, you're using property cards from the first phase to acquire money cards. This is a pretty easy game that's very easy to explain. It's a very popular filler game, that's also very easy to explain to new players. Definitely worth a look.

RATING: 7
Grade A auction game filler! 

This game played just as well with 4 as it did with 6. I couldn't see 5 being any different. I would probably suggest Scripts and Scribes with 3 or 4 though. 

I've played with both bidding rulesets and the Uberplay bidding is definitely better. With "match or raise" bidding the game just goes longer, and one of the best virtues to For Sale is the speed of the game. Skip the extra bidding and keep the speed at its highest. 

9 to 8. Raj plays the second half of this game better, so I may just break out Raj instead of this game. 

8 to 7. Scripts and Scribes is better for 4 than for sale and plays in about the same time.

RATING: 8.5
Auction game, inferior to Modern Art for strategy, but better as a fun filler for non-gamers.  Very accessible to new players, and the blind bidding in the second phase of the game leads to agonizing choices, similar to 6 Nimmt.

See my pictorial review here: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/378346

Own the Uberplay edition.

Different editions had different rules about rounding.  The designer Dorra [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/1875557#1875557]is quoted here[/url] as saying that when rounding you pay the higher amount to the bank and keep the lesser amount (the opposite way of playing is a result of a mistranslation in the German), see also [url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/21973322#21973322]here[/url], and [url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/21974828#21974828]here[/url].  Another interesting suggestion is this: "Actually, I prefer no rounding at all. You bid in $1000 increments, but use $500 chits for change. If you are down to your last $500 you have nothing left to bid."

RATING: 8
I used to stay far away from auction/bidding games, so when I once saw this game for sale for not even 4 euros, I didn't buy it. When later, after having experienced more and more mechanics, I found out this game rocks, I couldn't find it for under 20 euros. So it took me a while longer until I finally managed to buy me a not too overpriced copy. And it got played pretty soon after it.

I must say it's exactly what I expected it to be. A light filler game in which luck plays its part, but tactical play makes a great deal as well. And two different auction rounds. It should result in close scoring as well, though so far the same player won both games and one by quite a margin. He liked the game most, especially the opening round. While I personally prefer the second round, the blind bidding one. But overall, I like it and rate it an 8.

RATING: 6
FOR SALE ist ein klassisches Versteigerungszockspiel aus vergangenen Tagen, in denen das Immobiliengeschäft noch weniger kapitalverrucht war als in der heutigen, maßlosen neoliberalen Wirklichkeit. FOR SALE unterteilt seine Versteigerung in zwei gleiche Teile: Phase 1 Hauskauf, Phase 2 Hausverkauf. Und wer zum Schluß die höchste Schecksumme gescheffelt hat, ist der Sieger. Die Wertsteigerung mit Ziel einer positiven Handelsbilanz bildet Hab- und Geldgier im Kapitalismus gut wenn auch unabsichtlich ab. Was nur als friedliches Spiel gedacht ist und auch nichts anderes sein will, lässt sich heute als spielerisches Lehrstück für die oberste Prämisse im Kapitalismus begreifen: Profit über alles! FOR SALE ist ein gutes Beispiel, dass man im Spiel Dinge, Systeme und Verhältnisse ganz anschaulich und einfach begreifen kann, was sogenannte Wirtschaftsexperten und Finanzelite in ihrem umständlichen Geschwafel zu verschleiern versuchen.

RATING: 8
Bought May 2013.

Philistine! I hear you say. Heathen! How could you rate such a light and silly game so highly?

Well, show me another game that's so simple, so pretty, so successful with new players, and still fun after hundreds of plays, and I'll reconsider. This is the one game that no-one will ever object to playing.

Surely one of the best games ever designed. Quick and fun. Beautiful illustrations on the cards. Every group I've taught this to LOVED it, and I would teach it to anybody, even that wrinkled old northerner sitting in the corner supping a stale pint of Bass who says he'd rather eat his grandmother than play a bloody board game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The archetypal "filler game" really lives up to the expectations I had going in. It scales well up to six players, provides interesting (and sometimes difficult) decisions, plays very quickly, and provides the perfect balance of luck and skill for a game of this type. 

The illustrations are delightful and provide a great hook (even though the thematic integration is very thin). Players may even value cards more highly than they ought to simply because they like the dwelling (or even the pet) depicted. 

An experienced player has a definite edge in terms of card valuation, but the blind bidding element creates enough chaos that even a new player can pull off a surprising win. There are a lot of different elements that work very well together here, which makes it enjoyable even to experienced gamers. And yet this is one of the first games I would pull out to introduce someone to "those weird games" they've never heard of. 

Cheap, portable, accessible, fun, strategic, and scale-able. It's one of those games that just does everything right. Even so, I can't imagine this being anyone's favorite game. It's a great diversion or introduction, and succeeds mightily as a filler game, but after a few plays you will probably be craving something with a bit more substance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[Initial Impression: 8]
A fast and fun auction game, with a plausible economic theme.  The first round involves players bidding money chips to "stay in" (i.e., try to take a higher value real estate card) or passing to take the lowest card.  The second round involves blind bidding using the real estate cards to obtain checks that will score at the end of the game.  It's easy and fairly intuitive to explain, quick to play, and involves some bluffing and psychology, especially in the blind bidding round. Be frugal in buying and shrewd in bidding to become the richest real estate magnate of all.

RATING: 7
This is perhaps the best filler, ever, to pull out when you need to fill time, or to introduce folks to a little bit more complicated game, or to be silly, or all of the above, simultaneously.

Bluffing, guessing, auctions, it's all here in a short little game that can be played in minutes, and then probably played again (and again).

The components of this edition are fine, but basically you have a half deck of cards with houses, a half deck with checks and a stack of chips- this packs very small, and requires no board.

I rate this a 7, not because it isn't fantastic, but because there is only so much that one can get- or wants to add- in a game like this one.

But it's practically perfect for what it is.

RATING: 8
This is a great little auction game!  Every time I have introduced it, to gamers and non, they have stopped at the 2nd round and said "wait a minute, I see where this is going!"  I call it "Flip this House", and only explain the first half until we all have our house cards, and then explain the reselling part.

The down side, early big bids by new players can seriously hamper them later.  It takes a few turns for players to see the advantage of being the second-highest bidder.  That being said, it is a simple auction game that can be played in less than 20 minutes.  It obviously can't hold up to a whole evening of game playing.

RATING: 7
A "stupid fun" game, I suppose, without the fun. Yeah, there's probably some thinking involved, but I don't have the energy to figure this one out. I'd rather not waste my time on such stupid filler games.

Update (7/11/09): Well, when you've got 20 minutes and six people, and can't decide what to play... this may fit the bill. I've upped it to a '5', because there are elements of card trick taking. We had a pretty close game with 6P. Not too bad.

Update (06/18/10): After playing this a number of times since my last comment, I now appreciate the simplicity of gameplay, and depth of strategy, all within a 20 minute timeframe. A perfect filler. Upping to an "8".

Update (1/7/11): Hasn't seen much play lately. I couldn't stand my last game I played of this, especially when playing nothing but light games all day. I'm bouncing around with the verdict on this one. Dropping it to a '7'.

Update (02/02/2014) 14 plays: with 3-players, there is not enough variety of cards per turn to make the bidding interesting. It's just another way to kill the time until all the players show up. That's not saying much about this design. Holding precariously at "7".

RATING: 6.33
There’s a whole subclass of games that get high marks as “filler” games—5 to 15 minutes of 5 to 15 minutes’ worth of fun. They deliver, but they don’t put out.

For Sale is widely rumored to be among this group, and I just don’t see it. I like it better than [thing=917]Rack-O[/thing], and indeed it fired Rack-O for me. Like in Rack-O, you’re trying to choose where to stick in your cards to get them in the right position; unlike Rack-O it has peppy art and a double layer of auctions.

Bidding for property cards in the first round is reminiscent of the popular Rack-O draft variant; trying to put your oar in at just the right times in the second round is like trying to reassemble a spilled card catalogue: You make an educated guess about where your card corresponds to the overall scheme of things and hope for the best. At least that’s how I put together card catalogues.

For Sale isn’t the filler to end all fillers, and at my table it paled in comparison to [thing=220]High Society[/thing]; but I can’t deny I get a certain enjoyment out of playing it from time to time, and it’s worth keeping to play with the kids early on.[color=#ffffff]a9Z4MnAx[/color]

[color=#777777]mbadge = NONE[/color]

RATING: 6
One of my favorite introductory games!  The opening Phase allows players to duke it out, head to head, surpassing one another's bids.  It plays out almost like a hand of Texas Hold'Em, with people using their cash stack to knock others out of the bidding.  Then, Phase 2 kicks in and the mind games start..."what are they gonna put down for this check?  Low Card?  High Card?  Is it worth it to me to burn my 27 for a $12k check?"  If you are trying to convert any of the massive number of Hold'Em players out there to Euros, I recommend you start with this.

RATING: 8.2
So far, I haven't met anyone who does not enjoy this game.  I like the quick 10-15 minute play time and the unique market mechanics.  If there is a flaw, I would say it has to do with turn order.  The combination of random card drawing with turn order can put players into a very unbalanced situation.  A couple of nasty turns can pretty much knock a player out of contention.  That said, game play is pretty much guaranteed to be less than 15 minutes, so the misery is short lived.  

While strategy is important in this game, randomness is also very high.  I'd say it is darn near balanced on this continuum.  The turn order aspect knocks it down a few notches in my overall rating; however, it is tons of fun, easy to learn and play, affordable, compact and transportable, and repayable.  I recommend it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very enjoyable auction game, with plenty of tension and tough decision making throughout.  Players are trying to get a hand of high value properties in order to sell them at the end of the game to get the best prices available.  The fact that you are bidding to not have to take the worst card on the table in each round makes it interesting, as you have to judge when is the best time to pull out from the auction and cut your losses.

The endgame round is fun, as you are second-guessing your opponents' choices of property to sell - the player who played the highest value property gets to pick first and the player who played the lowest value picks last.

The game needs a few people in order to play it well, but it's worth it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Excellent light bidding/auction game. In the first half of the game players acquire property cards. In the second half, those same properties are sold, hopefully as dearly as possible. Most money at the end of the game wins.

Very much in the same style as Knizia's various bidding games, but simpler and faster. Comparable with Knizia's High Society in depth and playing time. Nice mix of analysis and luck. Good, fast filler. As a filler, easily a '9' or '10', so my rating merely reflects my bias in gaming.

Initial Rating: 6.0 (January 2013)

Good light game. I have played it twice now and it has begun to grow on me, I love auction games. The game can be explained under 3 minutes, have lots of hard decisions and it plays very quickly, also it has a surprising (and satisfying depth). It can accommodate 6 players without adding significantly to the play time, a huge plus for me. The art in the cars is ok and adequate. 

This is the perfect game to play with the family or to attract new players to our hobby.

In conclusion, great filler, I believe it will go up in my ratings with more plays. I will always carry it with me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
One of kids favorites! The game is played in two halves, buying houses then selling houses. Everyone starts with the same amount of money and properties equaling the number of players are turned face up. On your turn you have two options bid (more than the previous bid) or pass (take half you bid back and take the lowest valued property still available). If you are the last person you get the best property on the table but don't get any of your bid back.
This continues until all the houses in the deck have been bought.
Then cards with a monetary value are placed on the table (equaling the number of players). Each player chooses a property card and reveals it at the same time. Again the highest valued property gets the greatest money cardand so on. After all the rounds of selling houses are over the person with the most money wins.
I recommend this one with younger kids to teenagers. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Really good filler game that I would happily play. The game has two phases. The first phase involves bidding on different types of real estate. The second phase is selling off the real estate. This is done by revealing checks with dollar amounts and then each player takes one of her real estate holdings and all players simultaneously reveal the properties. The person who revealed the most valuable property takes the most valuable check and so on. This continues until all the checks have been collected. The player with the most money in checks at the end wins the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.8
I got this from BGG secret santa this year! I taught it to some friends over for dinner, ranging from casual to non-gamers, and all really enjoyed it. I used Tom Vasel's tip of first just teaching and playing the first half, then teaching and playing the second half; that worked well. This is a light, intuitive auction game that has won a permanent place in my rotation. Playing it for the first time is particularly fun as we see each property for the first time; the art is really clever (ranging from a box to a space station), and the artist has put in all kinds of little details like a relevant animal on each property. As much as I enjoyed it, I don't see it ever going higher than 8/10 due to the lightness.

Update:
After 7 plays, downgrading from 8.2 to 6.8.
Mostly due to two factors:
1. It's really just too light for me.  When I want to play light casual games with non-gamers, there are others I'd rather play than this, like Incan Gold.
2. To do well, you really have to remember (in the 2nd phase) who has already played what.  I don't really enjoy memory mechanisms, it feels too much like a chore.

So those two factors mean this one is probably headed for the trade pile.  The art really is nice though, it's my favorite part of the game.

Update: Traded away in Math Trade for Fearsome Floors.

RATING: 8
For Sale is the ultimate buy low, sell high game. That's all it is. It becomes indecently fun, though, when you're forced to manage your modest starting cash over several turns, trying to stick other players with over-expensive bids while grabbing the best deals. The game really depends on the turn sequence and you have to concentrate on your place in the wheel. You can play well, but the variety of properties to buy / checks to collect, combined with the anticipation of 3-5 other players (I wouldn't play with fewer than four and recommend up to six) makes it impossible to make perfect moves. Simple, addictive, replayable, and charming. The artwork does a LOT to encourage easy-going table-talk. Along with No Thanks!, this is your best bet for gaming in under 20 minutes. Recommended. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This is a quick filler about reading your opponents, a Vizzini-type game if there ever was one. This is my go-to 10 minute game.

Favorite mechanism: what I call the "split". In a given set of cards, what matters is not the absolute value, but their relative value to one another: bidding is much more important if the cards are 1-3-4-22 than 26-27-28-29; the same can be said of the cheque cards.

RATING: 8
A light, easy-to-understand game that has two parts - an open auction in which you are strongly constrained by the small amount of money you have at your disposal, and in which passing may pay off in the second auction - and a closed auction in which you must decide how to make best use of your weak cards as well as your strong cards.

A great little filler, engaging, fast, and enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Game is accessible, fast and clever. The first part is a normal auction with a little twist where only the winning players pays all their money while others get the keep the half of their bid. So you want to go out of the round not as the last person but the second to last one. In other words you want to win until the end where you want to be second. The second part is also quite clever. You need to bid high if the variance is big on the money cards otherwise you get rid of your weak cards to make decent money even with the garbage you have. This game is brilliant and with a price like that every gamer should own it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
As I age and find that my predictable time to play games with the same people wanes I'm finding that the criteria for what makes a "good" game is changing. A good set of criteria for a good game that's going to make it to the table, in my mind, looks something like this now:

1) Can I teach the game to someone that's never played it in 5 minutes?

2) Are there rules that can be forgotten or misunderstood that could break a game for someone, new or old?

3) Does it play quick enough that no one's going to be stuck playing a game they hate for 3 hours?

4) Does it have enough meat such that people feel their victory or defeat was the product of their decisions, even of they come from inexperience?

The best game is the one you play. Die Macher's fabulous - for the 2 times I've played it, each of which took the grater part of an afternoon between the game and learning the game. I've probably played For Sale a hundred times or more and have never had a single person _hate_ it. It's not a game a friend calls me up itching to play, either. Expensive for a deck of cards, but a great value based on the amount of time I've spent playing it.

For what it is there are few, if any, games that do what For Sale does better. Filler games never get the respect they deserve, and that's really sad.

[size=12][b]Why I like For Sale:[/b][/size]
+ If I have to play an auction game then I'd rather play a filler because I find this category quite boring. 
+ There is high player involvement
+ There is alright replayability
+ The game is easy to teach
+ The game is short

[size=12][b]Why I dislike For Sale:[/b][/size]
- The game lacks meaningful decisions and I'd rather play a push your luck filler game than an auction filler game

RATING: 7.75

It's one shortcoming is a lack of depth, but I don't think this is an intentional goal of the game. It is unashamedly what it is... a filler.

I wouldn't want this to be the main course of a board gaming night, but I'll definitely play it once (or twice) as an entree or dessert.

RATING: 8
Nice filler: not unique or anything, but because one game takes just 15 minutes and this IS auction game - I like it.

Many players like to compare this and High Society / Einfach Tierish, so here is mine opinion too: I think High Society is a lot of better than For Sale: 
1) In High Society, money is VERY important: if you have least money, you'll automatically loose! Little detail, but it really makes the game much much better.
2) In High Society, you don't know when the game ends: It might end after only few cards has been auctioned OR the game takes more rounds because the 'game-end' card is in the bottom of the deck.

RATING: 7
Great filler game in general and beyond that a great pure auction game with the interesting twist of having 2 seperate gmae phases.

The only somewhat negative aspect I can think of is that rounds with cards that have bigger value gaps are more interesting (for example: 2, 5, 25, 28 instead of 15, 16, 17, 18), but that random variance is an inherent part of the random card draw and over time it balances itself out.

RATING: 8
A clever two act game. Both acts work to create tension at decision points. The second act has a clear change of pace, moving the dramatic arc forward, but is similar enough that it doesn't break the flow and energy.

This is a great game of positioning, bluffing, and playing/reading other players.

You always feel like you're making meaningful decisions, but you never have enough control to let skilled play guarantee a win, keeping the game interesting to all, and making it a great experience for a broad audience.

RATING: 10
A very quick "filler" bidding game in two phases, where first half is a progressive turn-based bidding/auction to acquire currency (checks) so that, in second phase, you use your checks for simultaneous bidding/auction to buy one realty from each community lot of house cards.  It's crazy fun! but quick, light.  The key trick lies in anticipating what your opponents might do.  

My rating is for my preferred original edition rules, which you can simply edit/use with the new Uberplay edition components...

RATING: 8
Just about everyone I've introduced this to demands to replay it immediately.  And why not?  The games are 10 minutes long and full of a surprising amount of tough decisions and twists.

This is a top-three gateway game for me.  Great way to introduce heavier auction games or risk-management games.

Neither the first phase nor the second phase is very interesting on its own, but the combination of the two -- knowing that phase one is preparing for phase two, and playing phase two while remembering what everyone has collected in phase one -- is extremely fascinating.

By any metric -- sales price, box size, or playing time -- this game provides an absurdly high return on investment.

The nice theme puts it over the top for me.  I find the progression from cardboard box to space station really amusing.  Also love the little animals visible in each card.  Bonus:  can you find the animal in the Space Station card?

RATING: 9
A very simple and quick game, the play is broken down into two stages.  Everyone starts off with the same amount of money.  In the first round, you use your money to bid on property cards.  However many players there are (need at least 3), you lay that many cards out.  Property cards have point values on them.  Once these have all been bid on (every player will have the same amount of cards), the second round begins where you use your property cards to bid on monetary value cards. At the end, everyone has unloaded their property cards and the winner is whoever has the most money. Quick, fun and it helps to have a small player screen (I used the box lid) so no one knows how much money you have remaining.

RATING: 8
A fun 15 minute auction game is a hard thing not to like (unless you’re one of those weirdos that hates auctions ;). With not one but two different auctions packed into that time frame, For Sale is doubly difficult to hate.

Why For Sale succeeds so well, though, is how the auctions are connected to each other. The winnings in the first auction are the currency you use in the second auction. This, the random drawing of auction slates (each player wins one card form each slate) and the blind bidding (which I normally only tolerate, but quite like in this instance) introduce a nice level of uncertainty, and makes the game much more replayable that it might seem at first blush. The blind bidding also works nicely thematically, as you are, mechanically, bidding on cheques (lots of VPs) using properties you bought in the first auction. It takes a second to twist your brain around it, but it makes good thematic sense. When you hear the game described, it seems like it should be pretty calculable, but in practise you have to think on your feet quite a bit.

One small note: The rules call for randomly removing a few cards from each of the decks at the start of the game, even when the number of players allows for an even number of auction lots. While I don’t think removing 2 cards or so from the deck for some uncertainty is a bad idea, this is too many for my taste, and I prefer to play with the full deck in these cases.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.51
Comments entered on 02 Apr 2010:

For Sale is an interesting two-phase game. The first phase has players using their limited funds to bid and purchase the "houses" that come up for sale. The second phase has players using their purchased houses to sell for checks which garner the victory points needed to win.

This light game is played frequently because it's fast and easy to teach. It is the game that has been favored the past year or so when I get a chance to get together with Tim, John, and Mike.

RATING: 7
One of the better filler games in the market.  It's a game with enough randomness and rules light for anyone to enjoy.  Despite the light nature of the game, it doesn't prevent it from becoming a forgettable experience like other filler disposable games.

Every bid you make in this game has huge consequences towards the end goal.  Every dollar spent, every card acquired, and every currency card you've won is not a decision you can simply ignore.  Every round has just enough tension to be recognized and respected while not being overbearing for the player.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
great filler
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I'd been meaning to try For Sale for years, and I'm so glad I did. This is a perfect filler game: easy to learn and teach, and quick to play, but it has teeth and some meat to it.

The decisions in both rounds are interesting. The small set makes card-counting pretty accessible, and it helps to do so. (I'm average at that -- at best -- and can do just fine with it.) But you also have to read your opponents, assess what's on the table in relation to what's come before, what you're holding, what's still to come, and what might be in the kitty.

I can see why this is a classic.

RATING: 6.5
This game is better than the rating I gave it. For Sale is a game that does one thing, but it does that one thing well. The reason I have it rated slightly lower than it's potential (probably around an 8 or so) is because the one thing it does well isn't something that piques my interest. I'll still play it regularly, especially if someone asks.

For Sale simply revolves around bidding for things. It's not real-time, so there's nothing crazy going on. In fact, it devolves to some number-crunching for a good majority of the game. So basically, if bidding and medium-level number-crunching sounds like your slice of heaven, this will probably be about an 8 or 9 for you.

RATING: 8
Excellent game! This is likely the most accessible filler I've ever played. Player enjoyment is virtually guaranteed.

Update 4/27/14: Now that I've played this game with 2, 3 and 4, I definitely prefer it with 3. With 4 players, you are bidding against 3 other players. No matter how good you are at reading others, it's impossible to know what they are bidding. Also, the more players you have, the more you have to keep track of (in the way of counting cards). I appreciate that the small amount of strategy that this game has, and by more and more players, I think that strategy is increasingly diluted. Therefore, if you want a more strategic game, play with 2 or 3. If you want a more wild and certainly more random game, play with 4-5. 

BEST WITH 3!

Update 3/19/15: I can explain this game in 90 seconds and there is a good amount to chew on and enjoy. That alone makes it worthwhile in my opinion.

RATING: 8
For Sale is perhaps the very best pure auction game of all time.  It's also quite simple.  The game has two phases.  In the first phase players bid on housing properties with their starting money.  In the second phase players bid again, this time for cheques of various values, with the properties won in the first phase.  Whomever has the most money (a combination of cheques and left over starting money) wins the game.

It's that easy.  Children can play this game.  Yet it's such fun to bid and try to outmanoeuvre your opponents.  The artwork on the property cards is fun to look at as well.  Properties range from lowly out-houses, and igloos all the way to castles, and even a space station!  For Sale is maybe the only game I feel confident about saying belongs in everyone's collection.

RATING: 8.5
Fun, light auction game combined with trick taking.

Initially players start out with a pile of cash to spend bidding for different properties which are ranked 1 (the lowest of the houses - like a cardboard box) to 30 (the most impressive space station).  Any money left over is kept to the end of the game and counts towards victory.

Once everyone has their starting hands of property cards, players then simultaneously each play one per turn to try and claim differing cheques which are up for grabs at the same time.  A challenge to figure out whether to play high to get the valuable cheques, play low to burn the less useful properties on one's hand or play mid range and hope to clean up if everyone else has played low.

Great for playing with new players.  What it does it does fantastically well.

RATING: 7
For a short, light filler game with a lot of luck (on a per hand/round basis, at least), [i]For Sale [/i] involves a number of intriguing decisions.  It also has the highest fun/minute ratio of any game.

During the auction phase, it takes a bit of finesse and forward planning to avoid getting stuck in a situation where you've committed money and end up having to pass and take a crappy card.  Bidding/raising is not as easy as taking the previous bid and adding $1000.  Often choosing another value is much more advantageous.

Players simultaneously select which properly to put on the market during the selling phase, and knowing which to choose is rarely obvious.  It is helpful if you can remember which players own which properties (or at least which have those with the highest values) and how your opponents tend to play.  Will your opponent always throw out a 30 when a $15,000 card appears, or will he greedily bid lower, knowing that you are going to bid low since you "know" that he is going to bid high?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Game in a Nutshell: Use some money to bid on different valued properties in an auction. Then, in a blind bid, sell your properties for checks while trying to turn the biggest profit.

Played as: 4,5,6 player

Pros: I read for a long time this was a quintessential filler and now that I’ve played, I have to agree. Easy but satisfying. The two phases compliment each other well-- the first being a more tactical auction with interesting consequences for staying in or passing, followed by a simultaneous blind bid in the second round which leaves room for players to get in each others heads a bit. I love the Uberplay artwork (also carried into the Gryphon ed.) which keeps it light and unintimidating. It’s the kind of auction game anyone could like.

Cons: There’s a certain luck element as to turn order in the first round auctions that restricts your control of your options (more present the more players you have). There’s also a possibility that one bad decision can affect you in the long run like running out of money in the first round or playing a key property at the wrong time in the second essentially devaluing the rest of your hand by proxy. Kind of overpriced for what you get in the box.

Other:  I would have made this an automatic buy if wasn’t currently so stinking overpriced. Maybe the game-play makes it worth what they’re asking, but the component mass certainly doesn’t.  That just bothers me.

Best Use: In case you didn’t already know, a fantastic 20 minute filler.

RATING: 6
This is a quick and simple game of trading properties. The game is played in two phases, in the first phase players bid on properties revealed on the table, choosing either to up the current bid or to pass and withdraw half their bid (if any) rounded up and take the lowest valued property currently on the table. This continues until the property deck is depleted, and then in the second phase players play their properties secretly in order to win the most money possible, determined by the money cards revealed on the table. The higher valued property you play compared to your opponents the higher valued money card you win this round. Whoever has the most money at the end of the game wins the game.

This is a fun little game all about figuring out your opponents moves and adjusting your own accordingly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An excellent auction game where play goes around and players bid on property cards using a set bankroll. If they pass on a bid, they take the next lowest value property on the market, but they get half their current bid back. The winner gets the highest value property on the market, but pays their entire bid. The second half of the game has players selling their properties to attain the highest value "cheque" cards. Cheques and leftover money is tallied at the end, and whoever has the most wins. It's a neat double auction where winnings from the first half are used to bet on the points in the second half. The real key seems to be judging how aggressive to be when spending your money.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Brilliant auction/bluffing card game.  Can be played with practically anyone, regardless of their affinity for board games.  Great filler that near enough everyone should own.  Go get it!

RATING: 7
For Sale is a very easy to learn and very quick playing filler in the same category as High Society, No Thanks! and Loco. They are all fast playing, light on rules and all pack a deceptive level of tricky decision making into a small and very efficient package. 

Bidding too high or too low in the 1st stage can hurt as much as not having the good cards to obtain cash in the 2nd stage. The game really is about balance and picking your spots carefully to get the best value possible for your limited assets. Our first 3 player game of this ended 75,000-72,000-68,000. A close contest and one that made me a quick fan of the game.

An easy choice to get to the table when waiting for people to arrive for a longer and more complicated game.

RATING: 7
For Sale is the finest filler game that I've played.  In only fifteen minutes, it includes a lot of important decisions and some interesting tactics.  I like how the two phases work so well with each other, and how high valued houses aren't necessarily the best.  It's all about selling your houses for more than you bought them more than selling high valued houses.

The first phase can become a little rote at times, as in my group the bids almost always go 1, 3, 5, 7, pass.  Since bidding an odd number is in all ways better than an even, it's almost as if you are bidding at levels.  But depending on the houses themselves, it can be advantageous to switch up the bidding slightly.  As a result, if I have only fifteen minutes, I don't think there's another game that I'd rather play.

RATING: 5
Got this as a quick to learn, quick to play game for my daughters (9 and 12) and me.  Fits that bill supremely well as we can rattle off a game in 10 - 15 minutes.  Actually, that is what I like most about this game -- the speed at which a complete start-to-finish can take place AND the game manages to do this with a cute story arc thrown in.  It's like a game of spit but with a tangible story of buying low and selling high.  After one game my 12 y.o. was cackling over selling the dog house for $11 K and bemoaning the sale of a mansion for $3 K.  My younger daughter was more interested in collecting her favorite houses...and this strategy has won the majority of our games so far!

Edit: -1 because its half-life was very, very short.  For whatever reason I'd rather play a game of cards than this game.

Plays: 10+

EDIT: Someone pointed out in the forums that For Sale can be used to play a variant of No Thanks! a filler of a similar level, so I appreciate that. Overall For Sale has gotten a little stale for me, but I think playing different games with this set can be fun (like No Thanks or Kobayakawa). I also do like the pretty pictures and the theme more than the pictures on the other games (BTW, this is the only board game other than chess that I played my mother in) so I really appreciate it.

Trading because its just okay. It doesnt get me in the mood too often and hasn't really blown away anyone I've shown it too. I like auctions and the art, but I think I'd prefer Ra or Dream Factory for a bit more depth in my auctions beyond just the pretty art.

RATING: 8
Can't figure out why my wife doesn't rate this game as highly as I do. Also, I've found over time that it's better to teach this game backwards. When I've started by showing players what happens in the second phase, they have a much easier time understanding what their trying to accomplish in the first phase. I realize that I've just made the rules sound complicated. Nothing could be further from the truth. This game is so simple. But many folks just don't enjoy the anxiety of making choices for which they don't fully understand the potential ramifications. Anyway, this is a great game that I think belongs in every collection.

RATING: 8
[b]For Sale[/b] was received in a math trade (9/21/14).

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]This has become one of my families favorite fillers especially as an opener for family game night. In reality this game has stood the test of time for 18 years, I still haven't played a similar game that I've enjoyed more (Biblios comes to mind and in my opinion isn't nearly as good).

I can't recommend this game enough, it's been a hit with gamers, my family and our extended family and friends.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- Easy to teach, you can teach as you play (only explaining one phase at a time).
- I can see this becoming a staple when beginning or ending a game night.
- A great family game that can be a great first step in learning bidding/auction games.
- Awesome week night filler, we can get a 4p game in under 20 minutes.[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
50+ plays. A high quality 15 minute opener that always proves enjoyable. 

The game leads off with 4-6 auctions depending on number of players. The auction feel is different from other games due to the matching mechanism, so it's not so much a question of raise or not, but more one of if I match, how likely is it that other people will bail out - and am I happy paying full price if everyone else bails out and gets half their money back. So the auction moves gradually up from auto-match to decision time. 

The second half provides a simultaneous play aspect to turn the houses bought in the first halve into cash. Again, because it's only 4-6 rounds, there's a fun denouement feel which is perfect in length. 

The game remains a solid winner for us.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
For Sale is a marvelous achievement in game design. The double auction mechanism is simple and intriguing and satisfies both a short term and long term satisfaction itch in any gamer.
 
A typical game will have 5-7 rounds in each of two phases. In the short term, you get the thrill of winning or the agony of defeat over 10-14 hands. In the long term, you get to see if you can master value investing, buy low/sell high compared to your fellow gamers.

This game is inexpensive, accessible for children, quick to set-up, quick to play, with an element of mind games and memory. I find that my eldest son and I play against the player, as in poker, moreso than just the current hand, which adds depth.

My favorite part is trying to play a poor hand well. The only possible criticism is that due to the auction order some first phase hands are just not worth pursuing if you are one of the last two players to submit a bid. This forces a player to learn how to play weak cards well.

RATING: 9.25
Fantastic little game that is ridiculously easy to learn and very quick to play.  All of my games have been 3 player but I'll bet it plays equally well, if not better, with a full compliment of players.

Great production, small box format, rulebook - clear, concise, to the point.  You can learn how to play in 5 minutes from the book before even opening up the cards.  (well, I did anyways)

Once you do, you'll find a colourful, enjoyable bidding game played in two distinct rounds.  The first - buying properties - where players purchase the face-up properties in a clockwise auction setting until one player passes and then s/he takes the lowest value card.  The round continues until all players either outbid or pass, leaving this highest-paying player the highest valued card on the table.

The second round involves selling the properties that have been won - against a new deck of 'value' cards.  These cards are laid out, and each player secretly chooses a property from those s/he has won the round previously, and the highest card played takes the highest valued cheque card.  At the end, whoever has the most money wins.

Beauty!  Simple!  Clever.  Most of this will be played for sure, excellent value within.

RATING: 6
This is a quick filler card game composed of two phases of auctions involving real estate trades. In the first phase, round cardboard tokens are used as money for the auction. The auction is a standard open auction going around clockwise till all but one player drop out. You can easily run out of coins, so don't go overboard bidding!

In the second phase, cards (representing checks) are used for auction. The cards each has a unique number on it and are used for the second round auction. This time blind auction is used in which players simultaenously play a card as their bid.

Easy to teach and quick to play, but still has some Subtleties in when to bid or drop out of the auction and also a little bit of keeping track of what cards other players have already played.
Very good as a filler.

RATING: 8
Ingatlanügynökök vagyunk, akik a játék első fázisában ingatlanokat vesznek a lehető legkevesebb pénzért, majd az ingatlanokat próbálják a legnagyobb profittal eladni.
Hogy néz ez ki?
Van 2x30 kártya, az első fázisban kiborítunk annyi lapot, ahányan vagyunk, majd a sorszámozott ingatlanokra licitálunk. Aki először kiszáll, az a megkapja az addigi licitjének a feléért (felfelé kerekítve) a legkisebbet, majd megy tovább a licit addig, amíg már csak ketten maradnak, akkor is elveszi félpénzért az utolsó előtti a második legnagyobb kártyát, míg az utolsónak be kell fizetnie az összeset a bankba a legjobb ingatlanért.
A második fázisban nem pénzzel, hanem az ingatlanjainkkal fizetünk a felborított csekkekért. Az nyer, akinek a csekkjei a legtöbbet érnek.
Hiába tűnik egyszerűnek, minden parti más és más és keményen lehet taktikázni is a legjobb lapokért!

RATING: 6.5
Simple, short and quick auction game. It's basically a game of chicken to see who is willing to stay in the longest and get the best property at the expense of paying full cost. The checks phase of the game also contains subtle strategy to outplay your opponents. One issue that I found was that it usually better to bet in increments of $2k. Since you keep half of your bet rounded down if you bow out, betting $1k and $2k yields the same cost unless you win the best property.

RATING: N/A
This is treated as something of a classic, and it's pretty easy to see why (although I guess the game has seen some small rules tweaks over the years as far as how the first-phase auction works). The first half of the game you acquire properties via an auction, and in the second half you sell those for money based on available money cards that everyone is simultaneously blind-bidding on with their property cards. Most money wins. Boom, done. It's easy to teach, easy to grasp, quick to play, and is clearly a top tier short game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Awesome filler.  A great push your luck/bluffing/bidding game that lasts 10 to 20 minutes.

The game is composed of two rounds.  In the first players bid sequentially on houses.  There are 30 numbered from 1 to 30.  The first player to pass takes the lowest valued house and pays half their bid and so on until only one player is left.  That player has to pay their entire bid for the highest card.

In the second round, check cards are revealed and players bid the houses acquired in the the first half of the game on the checks.  Houses are bid face down and revealed once all have bid.  Checks are worth $0 to $15,000.

The randomness of what cards come out together combined with attempts to outguess your opponents makes for a great filler game.  It's not quite as good as Liar's Dice.

RATING: 8

Edit (10.06.2017): Yesterday after Pandemic Legacy we played a quick round of this! I reminded me what a wunderful game this really is. In a 20-30 minutes time-frame it presents lighthearted but still tough decisions, and the tabletalk which occurs always during this game is fantastic. I can't imagine anybody who wouldn't enjoy this. 
But this highlight also my problem with the rating system on BGG. For what it is, it definately deserves a 10, but I cannot put it onto the same level as Great Western Trail, Mombasa or Le Havre. We are mostly into medium-heavy euros, but still I highly appreciate this game for what it gives to this hobby: one of the most accessible and best filler you can own.

RATING: 8
Wow! Sometimes I avoid simpler games like these because I don't see how something so casual can live up to the hype. For Sale exceeded my expectations by a long shot. It's casual and simple, for sure, but these are some of it's greatest strengths. In 30 minutes I learned the rules, taught it to two other players and played two full games. It's light on strategy, but there's enough here that two players could approach the game completely different and have a shot at winning. You will occasionally get the lucky draw and score a high card with your weak property, but smart card play rules overall. I dub this a new favorite filler game.

RATING: 8.8
Players: 3-6
Time: 15 min.

Card game with money chips and bidding mechanic. The game is played in two phases. In the first phase, players buy real estate (cards) of various values by bidding on properties. Once all properties are bought, the players then sell their properties in the second phase. The player with the most money at the end wins. A lot of interesting decisions packed in this little game.

RATING: 7
This game is simply fun.  

It's repetative and folks can grow tired of it but after really, a ton of plays (way more then I keep up with) it's still fun.

You first have to buy real estate cards by using a small allotment of coins you are given at the beginning via an auction mechanism with the other players and then once you have all your real estate cards, you use them to acquire the best sale price cards which are monetary denomination cards that at the end of the game will be tallied to determine the winner.

It's really quick and fairly simple but when you need such a thing it can be taught in a matter of minutes and most folks find it intuitive once they start.  Really a fun game.

RATING: 6.75
For Sale is a pure auction game. First you auction properties for money, then you auction cheques (worth money) for properties. At the end the person with the most money wins. The second auction is fairly obvious, you bid high when the distribution of cheque values is large, but the first auction is difficult - do you bid for high absolute values (26-30), when the spread is large, or for position (I want to be second in the bidding frequently)?

RATING: 8
A lesson in doing more with less. It's a bit of an exercise in gaming the players, per se, as multiple rounds of For Sale can give you an idea of who plays risky and who does not (or rather, assess the tendecies players WISH to convey), and so there is a bit of metagaming aspect to this. I like the interaction, I appreciate the quick play time, and I enjoy the opportunity for subverting high bids with cleverly timed and very profitable low bids.

RATING: 6
Combines open auction bidding with blind bidding. Supports six players, which is nice. The bid-raise auction takes a while to get used to, because it's difficult to know what to bid (especially with random cards removed from the deck), but with the right group  the blind-bidding phase at the end is instantly fun and has everyone laughing and animated. Interestingly, some groups seem to prefer the property auction to the blind bidding. 

A decent filler, but it won't change your life or anything. Strangely compelling nonetheless... must play one more game...

Update: Played a couple more games and my group (of overly analytical types) still liked it less than I did, so I traded it.

Update the second: Of course, as soon as they found out that I'd traded it they had a change of heart and keep asking for it. Grrrr.

RATING: 7.5
Good filler. Easy to learn. Strategy and bluffing opportunities. And fast! The game plays in two stages. Everyone gets some money to start. In the first stage, players go through several rounds bidding off the entire real estate deck (each round you put out N cards, where N is the number of players in the game). Real estate cards are valued from 1 through 30. In a round, you can either bid or pass; if you pass, you claim the lowest value card and take back half of your bid (if any, round up). When everyone but one has passed in the round, the remaining player wins the remaining (high) card at full price. Once all the real estate cards are distributed, the game enters stage two. The players go through several rounds bidding on check cards, like you did in round one, but you bid using the real estate cards and all bids are secret -- in other words, each player picks a real estate card and plays it face down, then all players reveal. The highest valued real estate card gets the highest valued check, and so on. Checks are valued from 0 to $15,000. This round goes even faster than the first. At the end, add up your checks and your cash to see who the winner is. I have to play a few more times to see if there's as much strategy to this game as it seems for such a little package. Fun. Thematically the game is a little dry, you're basically using numbers to bid on numbers, then using those numbers to bid on other numbers ...

RATING: 7
Very quick and playable filler. The game is split into two auctions: the first part has players bidding with money for buildings valued 1 to 30. The second auction segment has players using the buildings to blind bid for cheques. The player with the highest amount of money from cheques and currency wins.

The first part has elements of High Society and players should know when to bid high or when to pull out. The blind bidding part is about bluffing and very intense.

Good quick fun and an alternative to High Society.

RATING: 6
"For Sale" is a really great game for non-gamers and gamers alike. It's super easy to understand, teach, and play. However, 3-6 players are needed to play the game. I've had this game in my collection for awhile now and really enjoy it (even though I don't typically win at it), but outside of my gaming group, it's just me and my wife. The gaming group has a lot of filler games that we rotate through, so "For Sale" doesn't see a lot of table time. On top of that, games requiring 3 or more players can't be played with it just me and my wife. Imagine my surprise when I stumbled upon a solo variant for the "For Sale" game! It's called "[b][url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/556146/for-solitaire]For $olitaire[/url][/b]" (by kvenosdel) and it actually works extremely well. I've yet to beat "Jessica" (the imaginary AI player), but that doesn't mean that I'll quite trying. So, if you have "For Sale" sitting around and wish you could play it more, now you can. I highly recommend the solo variant. Have fun!

RATING: 8
 Very fast, very easy, very nice economic filler for 3-7 players. 

 Perfect to introduce new people to the hobby, although it's so easy they won't really form an accurate idea.

 Offers a nice challenge concerning how you will handle your resources in both phases- how to invest money in the beginning and how to cash out your investments in the end. You need to guess how your opponents will move and spend little as possible to claim greater benefits as possible.

RATING: 6
I think this is going to be a solid filler for years to come. If you aren't sure what a good "filler" game should be, play this to find out. For Sale is fun, quick, light, and contains both tactical and strategic decision-making - and an element of trying to second guess other players' choices. This game is never going to be the "event" around which a game night is organized, but its an outstanding starter/filler. The thing about "filler" games is, they may see table time only very infrequently. But I think we'll keep this one - our single "go-to" filler game.

In the first half of the game, players bid up properties in an effort to get the highest value plots at the lowest possible price. In the second half, players participate in a blind bid to secure the most money for their properties. I look forward to playing more of this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Everyone starts with the same amount of money.  Properties of varying values are auctioned, n at a time (where n= number of players).  So in a 4 player game, there would be 7 rounds of auctions, high bidder taking high valued property, and so on.  When all properties are auctioned, they are used in a blind auction to win cheques of differing denominations, n at a time.  Everyone secretly selects a property from their hand, and they are revealed simultaneously.  Highest valued property gets the biggest cheque, and so on.  Again, in a 4p game, there would be 7 rounds of blind bidding for cheques.  Most cash wins.  Play time is 15 minutes with rules explanation.  This is fast and exciting, and among the best fillers out there.
