RATING: 9.5
Just bumped this to an 8, and it may even go up to a 9.  It is almost as pure an auction game as you can get.  The first phase is an open-auction where timing is everything in that you can get half-price on almost anything, but will pay full-price if the timing is bad.  The silent auction of the second round is very entertaining, and even if you have a lousy hand from the first round, it's still entertaining to see what you can make out of it.  It seems that I'm almost always ending the night playing this one.

UPDATE: Have to move this to a 9, as I just really enjoy playing it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After several plays, this is a really good auction game! Easy to explain, fast to play, with plenty enough tension in the auctions.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
[u]SETUP[/u]
- Separate the cards by type; shuffle separately.
... 3er: Remove 6 random cards / deck
... 4er: Remove 2 random cards / deck
- Place the property deck face down.
- Give each player $18K money chips (2 x $2000, 14 x $1000)
... 5-6er: $14K (2 x $2000, 10 x $1000)
... Money is secret.

[u]PLAY[/u]
[i]Phase 1: Buying Properties[/i] (n-player game)
- Reveal n Property cards.
- Starting with random start player and going clockwise, players make a bid or pass. Whenever someone passes, they take the lowest valued Property card and half their bid (rounding down). 
- Continue auction until all properties are won. The last player pays full price and will go first next round.
- Keep purchased properties face down in front of you.
- Repeat above until all the property cards have been sold.

[i]Phase 2: Selling Properties[/i]
- Reveal n Cheque cards (valued $0-15,000)
- Players secretly choose one of their Property cards to sell; reveal simultaneously.
- In descending Property card order, players take the highest valued cheque still available. Discard used the Property cards; add Cheque cards to your remaining money chips (if any)

[u]END OF GAME[/u]
- Repeat Phase 2 until all checks have been taken.
- Winner = player with highest sum of Cheque cards and money chips. Tiebreaker = most remaining money chips. 
 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Super simple, super fast auction game.  The game consists of two phases.  In the first phase players bid to win property cards with numerical values on them.  In the second phase players play these property cards to win cards with monetary values on them.  The player with the most money at the end of both phases wins.

There's not much to this game at all but it does play in about 15 minutes and presents players with a couple of decisions to make.  My plays with this game so far have both been with 3 and I've heard and can see how this would be more interesting with the full complement of 6.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
"Try to get the highest numbered card here."
"Play the highest numbered card out of all the players to get the highest numbered card from this pile."

Quite easy to teach and pick up. It's really no wonder why it's remained so popular over the years. Simple and elegant is an apt description for it. It's strange how you can get so much enjoyment out of a game that's just chips, a deck numbered 1-30, and a second deck numbered 0 and 2-15 x2.

Got the travel edition and am quite pleased with how space efficient the box is. It accommodates sleeved cards (though it leaves no room for the chips) and leaves very little extra space. (It's also kind of scary how the non-travel version which has virtually the same components costs more than twice what the travel version does.)

The property deck is also useful for mapping it to No Thanks! and possibly other card games where the cards are simply numbered. For Sale also conveniently comes with chips, which are also useful for mapping No Thanks!.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game really is a perfect "filler", as long as you have the right number of players (I imagine 4 or 5 would be best).  It is extremely simple, plays shockingly quickly, and, most importantly, it's fun!  It's also the ideal auction game for a new gamer since the value of each item in comparison to the others is known outright (with auction games, it's often impossible for a new player to know the value of items that they are bidding on, and therefore how much they ought to bid).  The artwork is cute (in a good way!), my only quibble with the components being that players are supposed to keep their money secret yet no player screens are provided.  There are more interesting auction games out there (such as "Modern Art", but I don't think there are any that are more approachable or fast-playing than "For Sale".  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 3/5  Total: 7/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[Uberplay 05 edition] {Merchant, filler; auction, blind-bidding; optimal 4-6p} For Sale is a fast-paced filler with great player interaction and plenty of fun. It has a good combination of bidding and double-guessing. Since every player gets one house each turn, it doesn't have much down time because players won't complete aggressively and think too much in the bidding; esp. when someone has spent for the first few houses.

FS is unique in the sense that it involves 2 different genres of bidding. In the 1st round, players use limited 'money' to collect houses with open bidding and use the collected houses to bid for the check in the 2nd round. Therefore, even if a player collects a decent set of houses, he could not win the game because he cannot get high-value checks due to the chaos in blind bidding. (Some players are smart in open bidding but dull in closed bidding.) Each turn is repetitive but the game is fast enough and ends before every one gets bored.

Making decisions in FS is easy. The only difficult part is in the closed bidding; esp. when high-value checks are mixed with low-value/VOID checks. From my experience, it's better to get more middle-value houses than a handful of high- and low-value houses.  In addition, it can be nasty to block your left-hand players with high bid at the first round.

The graphics of the cards are very beautiful. I like that each house has its own illustration. It is of very good production - Uberplay is exceptional in producing high-quality card game!

[Update] There is an error in the rule of first edition.  The revised rule makes more sense.

RATING: 7.5
tl;dr: holds up

This is such a cool little game. First you use money to auction checks, then in the second half you use those checks to blind bid for properties of different values. It's so simple and effective. The fun of course comes from knowing who is holding what checks in the second half and anticipating when these might come out. Sometimes people save them for later and only crappy properties come up.
This all comes together very well in a game that will keep on hitting the table for years to come.

RATING: 4
I find this game a bit dull - I played several games against three other players and found   it a strangely detached experience. You make binary choices over a couple of rounds and cannot really influence or know your opponents actions. You still have no idea how you have done in the final round of bidding and then you win or lose by a low margin completely by chance. 

I think the final round (swapping properties for cheques) could be livened up by having a bigger range of cheque amounts. 

Overall this isn't for me. I like to play against my opponents and this just doesn't deliver.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
My one "can't miss" game for almost any group.  Everyone who plays it likes it.  After one game everyone gets it and wants to try again.
I've played it quite a bit and I am still not tired of it.

I think one of the things that makes this one great is that there are both in-game strategies and meta-game strategies that are both working at different levels yet are both grasped easily enough. People can sink their teeth into the process that is most fun to them and have an equal chance of winning. And if you are particularly poor at evaluating value (Auction Phase) then you have a chance to make up for it in the Selling Phase. It's almost like a gamers game and a party game in one. 

What I'm saying is that there are lots of ways to enjoy this game. It can be all things to all people.

Update: Raised rating from 8 to a 9.  This game should be in every collection. It is a great title to get non-gamers into more game-y thinking.  Scales well from 3-6 and I still haven't found a group who doesn't like it.  

It is simply one of my all-time favorites.

Update:  Still a 9. Everything I wrote is still true. I have had some pretty tough individuals (not just non-gamers, but ANTI-gamers) at the table who have enjoyed this one. 

From the standpoint of versatility this is nearly a perfect game.




Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 4
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 4
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 4
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 4


Overall - 4

RATING: 8
I am sold on this game.

This highly popular card game filler is one that lives up to it's lofty reputation.  It is a fast,  two stage auction affair,  that should appeal to most any gamer.  Any criticisms of the game,  although there aren't many,  can be quelled by it's short duration.  In the rare instances that a game falls flat.  you can just reload and shoot again.

It has been my experience that this game is always a worthwhile investment.  This is a sentiment,  that for the most part,   is unanimously shared by a number of players with a wide range of preferences.

So,  prepare to flip those cards - and hopefully flip those houses -  for untold millions,  or at the very least,  for more profit than the rest of your fellow real estate moguls.

RATING: 8.5
Great filler!

It has two distinc phases:

1 - Properties equal to the number os players are put for an open auction. The first that passes, get the one that has the lowest number. The second, the second lowest, until the winning bid get the higher number. This go on until every property is sold;

2 - Then, a number of money cards equal to the number of players are put for closed auction. So everyone chooses a property, then all of them are revealed in the same time. The property with the highest number gets the card worth more; the second gets the second most valued card and so on. This happens until all the properties are sold.

After these two phases, the player with more money, win!

It is easy to understand and it is great fun to play. The game is fast and tense, with laughs and curses - so all the good stuff.

The artwork is nicely done.

It's hard for me to grade a filler higher than 8, but For Sale sure is making me want to give it a .5 or 1 point more.

My review (in portuguese) is [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/827343/for-sale-resenha-em-portugues-review-in-portugue]here[/url].
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
You bid out of a smallish treasury to get properties, and then bid those properties to collect larger amounts of money. So it's two iterations of conversion, though in the first stage it is impossible to bid what something will actually be worth as you don't have that much money. This need to convert one thing into another as "losslessly" as possible is interesting.

But I don't like the chaos that results from the fact that all players are simultaneously trying to bid on three things.

...Up a bit. It's a fun game with others.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
As far as fillers go, this is a keeper. However, unlike many, I find For Sale significantly weaker than High Society as far as auction fillers go. While both games center around evaluating your opponents moves, the luck factor for For Sale is much higher, round after round, than High Society. The order of how checks emerge from the deck greatly influences property selection. While this is the basis for most of the game, I found the final phase of the game to be most chaotic and uncontrollably random. I was unable to even make educated guesses and if someone is adept at card counting this would most certainly break the game. Compared that with High Society, the single luck factor in that auction game is when the fourth red card will emerge. So, there is that single risk aspect to the game. When all is said and done, For Sale is still a decent filler if only as a filler, it will only be played every so often.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For Sale is a very competent auction-based filler game that plays in two stages. Players attempt to acquire desirable properties in the first stage by outbidding opponents, taking care not to overbid. In the second stage, the players compete to sell their properties to the best buyers in the market.

Components: The game comes with 30 property cards which are beautifully illustrated and quirky, ranging from space stations to castles to literally a box in an alley. The game also includes coins for money and money cards for the second stage of the game in denominations from $0-$15,000 which are very basic in design.

Ease of learning/teaching: The game mechanics are very simple to explain and pick up, even for non-gamers.

Theme: The game is an auction game at heart and the theme is skin deep. However, it does the job and provides set-ups for appropriate real estate jokes, which is all you can really ask for from a game of this weight.

Gameplay: The game plays fast and has plenty of depth despite its weight. It takes a few games to develop an intuition and strategy to bid correctly, and rounds of the game make for excellent lessons in rudimentary game theory. The game ends quickly enough to adjust your approach and set up for another round.

A fun, fast paced game with simple and straightforward mechanics.

RATING: 8

RATING: 9
Buy and sell real estate. There are 2 parts to a game: an initial "buying phase" where houses of different values are auctioned and players build their hands, and a second "selling phase" where these cards are played simultaneously in an attempt to win the highest valued checks. Player making the most money wins. Plays extremely quickly (15-20 minutes), really easy to teach and understand, and offers tough and interesting tactical decisions with bluffing along the way. Above all, this game is fun! The perfect filler, should be in every gamer's collection.

RATING: 7
A short and sweet game that's played in two parts. First you buy houses and then you try to sell them off. This game uses a blind bidding mechanic in both parts of the game which fit the game really well. Blind bidding has some strategy in it, but a huge portion of luck as well. This fits this particular light game though. Artwork is good and fitting and I even find it a bit funny (cardboard box for the cheapest house, space station for most expensive). Overall, I'd consider this a heavy filler that's definitely going to stay in my collection.

RATING: 7
'For Sale' is themed around buying and selling properties in order to make the most profit and be declared the winner. This is done over a series of bidding rounds.
Each player starts with the same amount of money, despite some initial confusion over this (!), and bids in sequence on the property cards displayed in front of them.
The person bidding the most will select the highest valued property (1 - 36?) and as each bid is made, there is an option to 'go higher' or pass as you cannot overbid!
Passing means that the player takes the lowest valued property , so over time, it may be that you could still get a reasonable property even if you pass later than someone else.
Passing also means that any bids you may have made are refunded to you in their entirety. The highest bidder pays their full bid, and all other purchases are halved.
I hope this is correct as my memory of the game is limited! Once all the properties are acquired, properties are used to acquire cheques of various sizes in the next phase.
This is done by secretly bidding a property based on the player's estimation of whether their bid will be enought to get the highest price and involves significant bluff and luck.
There are some void cheques, and I got one of those, but with the second lowest property value bid, that was hardly surprising! Knowing the spread of cheques values would help here.
Of course, that requires several plays, and this may not be a game for everyone. For me, it was alright, and the theme and mechanics played well enough, but I don't believe its replay value is good.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
If For Sale were only the first half of the game, then it would just be one more game in a long line of bidding games.  If it were only the second half of the game, then it would just be one more game in a long line of Raj games.  Put them together, though, and you have a neat game where the choices you make in the first half of the game determine how you play in the second half.  I think it's that transition that makes this game so enjoyable for me.

Count me among the folks who prefer the Ravensburger edition of the game over the uberplay/FRED edition.  For one, the ability to call a bid instead of raising it means that players can stay in the bid for a longer time, thus helping them stay in the game.  For another, the game should max out with 5 players instead of 6, because with 6, you don't have as many cards in hand for the second half of the game to stay as competitive, and pairing the 6 players with the inability to call a bid means that the player who starts a bid will likely be out of it by the time the bid has made it all the way around the table.  Lastly, the distribution of the checks in the Ravensburger edition makes more sense to me.

2 plays: 4 players (Ravensburger edition)
2 plays: 5 players (Ravensburger edition)
1 play: 3 players (uberplay edition)
3 plays: 4 players (uberplay edition)
3 plays: 5 players (uberplay edition)
4 plays: 6 players (uberplay edition)

---

Ravensburger ed.

RATING: 7
For Sale is a great family/filler game. It is about as simple to play as games get, yet the act of play is exciting. Between auctioning in the beginning and second-guessing your opponents during the cash-grab in the second half, players are always on their toes and trash-talking is second-nature.

Highly recommended for family gatherings as the game supports up to 6 players and plays in about 15 minutes.  By fast and frantic nature, it's a game that people generally won't want to play just once, so be prepared for bouts of 3, 4 or even more plays in a row.

A game that belongs in every collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Around 5 plays, with 3-5 players. 

Like: Easy to learn and easy to teach. The two phase structure of the game makes teaching new players particularly easy, since the two phases can be taught independently. There is unique and often amusing artwork for all 30 of the property cards which is quite engaging. I particularly enjoy the property flipping in the 2nd phase. It sets up some interesting scenarios of trying to guess what your opponents will play. It's also very satisfying to use a low-numbered property to snatch a high-value money card, or to force everyone else to take bad money cards by playing a high-numbered property. 

Dislike: The mechanics of the auction phase are a little strange, particularly with 5+ players. The auction is a partially all pay system, in which everyone except the winner of the auction pays half their bid rounded down. However, the rounding down has a massive effect because typical bids are in the $3-6,000 range, which gets rounded to $1-3,0000. There is thus little incentive to bid even numbers unless you expect your bid to be the final bid (so that you win the auction). This drives prices very high very quickly, since players will be incrementing by $2000 instead of $1000. With 5 players, what then happens is that once you pay a high price for a property (and thus get reduced to around $5000 or so), if the bidding starts a few players to your right, then the price will shoot up to a point that you cannot afford and you will be forced to take the lowest numbered property, even though you have plenty of money. With high player counts it is hard to defend against this unless you hold onto your money (since it's nearly impossible to control where the bidding will start or end unless you make a high bid), but then if all players are tight with their money, the game becomes quite boring. 

The other main aspect of the game I dislike is the strong memory aspect. For Sale immensely rewards tracking what cards have come out of the deck so far and knowing what properties each of your opponents has. This is exacerbated in the 5-player game since no cards are removed from the property or money decks, so that the last round of each phase will have a completely determined tableau of cards available at the end. I'm also not a fan of the last round of the property flipping phase, since it is played automatically (each player only has one property left which they must play). 

Overall: A solid, quick game which is easy to teach and has very charming artwork. The auction becomes a little funky with 5+ players. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The entire game is basically 2 rounds of auctions -- the first round is open bidding with money/coins where you bid on the highest value property. Your bid is visible to others as it's happening.  The 2nd round is selling your newly acquired properties for checks (money).  Each player simultaneously selects a property to sell and checks are assigned based on property values. The player with the most money wins.  It's easy to teach, bidding gets crazy, and it plays in about 15 minutes per game.  One of my favorite fillers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
{ P - [4] 5 6 }

The first round is often stagnant; each property has a ceiling price where bidding higher simply isn't worth it. Once bid, everyone else drops out unless they arbitrarily feel compelled to bid higher for the privilege of going first. The second round is very random and has this bluff / double-bluff issue where you draw a pool of say Void 3 5 13 and everyone knows the differential is huge and the 30 is coming out ... unless the people who don't have 30 trash low houses and the guy with 30 plays 15-20 instead. Ten minute filler, at least.

RATING: 10
After 5+ Plays: Very quick and very fun.  What more could you ask for?

After 10+ Plays: My favorite filler.  It's all interesting decisions and no busywork.  I'd play several rounds of this over a lot of longer games.

After 30+ Plays: This is just the perfect filler - I really doubt it will ever be surpassed.  No set-up or tear-down time, 20 minutes from opening the box to putting it back on the shelf, and yet it really feels like a complete game with interesting decisions.  Beyond the realm of fillers, I'd say only Ra is a better pure auction game.
